    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer16683388821339186722">Calvin is a rambunctious six-year-old whose manic antics threaten world peace. Hobbes is his stuffed tiger who comes alive when adults aren't around. The saga of their daily exploits won cartoonist Bill Watterson the coveted Reuben Award for "Outstanding Cartoonist of the Year." <i>Something Under the Bed Is Drooling</i> is a jewel.</span>

<div id="review_1622491365" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1622491365" />
    <a title="Joey Hines" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7794113-joey-hines"><img alt="Joey Hines" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329708379p2/7794113.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1622491365?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joey Hines" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joey Hines" href="/user/show/7794113-joey-hines">Joey Hines</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 0590062271" href="/book/show/77729.Something_Under_the_Bed_Is_Drooling">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7794113-joey-hines?shelf=comics">comics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7794113-joey-hines?shelf=j">j</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7794113-joey-hines?shelf=calvin-and-hobbes">calvin-and-hobbes</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1622491365" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5738987625608089679"><i>Something Under the Bed Is Drooling</i> contains some of Calvin's funniest moments, like when he is cast in a school play about nutrition:<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1461789534i/18911870._SX540_.gif' width='1000' height='' alt='' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>...which doesn't help his relationship with Susie:<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1461789534i/18911871._SX540_.gif' width='1000' height='' alt='' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>...as well as the first appearance of the Transmogrifier:<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1461789534i/18911872.jpg' width='1000' height='' alt='' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>But the best moment -- and a contender for my favorite moment in all of C&H -- is the story arc about the dying raccoon that Calvin finds in the woods:<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1461789534i/18911873._SX540_.jpg' width='1000' height='' alt='' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>What a stupid world indeed. And yet, "it's a magical world," as Calvin would later famously declare. The co-</span>
  <span id="freeText5738987625608089679" style="display:none"><i>Something Under the Bed Is Drooling</i> contains some of Calvin's funniest moments, like when he is cast in a school play about nutrition:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1461789534i/18911870._SX540_.gif" width="1000" height="" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>...which doesn't help his relationship with Susie:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1461789534i/18911871._SX540_.gif" width="1000" height="" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>...as well as the first appearance of the Transmogrifier:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1461789534i/18911872.jpg" width="1000" height="" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>But the best moment -- and a contender for my favorite moment in all of C&amp;H -- is the story arc about the dying raccoon that Calvin finds in the woods:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1461789534i/18911873._SX540_.jpg" width="1000" height="" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>What a stupid world indeed. And yet, "it's a magical world," as Calvin would later famously declare. The co-existence of these two statements gets at the heart of what C&amp;H is about. Calvin is both the ultimate cynic and the ultimate idealist. He is incredibly imaginative, but he is "no namby-pamby," as Pat Oliphant puts it in the foreword. He refuses to be bogged down by the pragmatic, conformist world around him, preferring to live as if the colorful fantasies in his head are real. And who's to say they aren't? My personal interpretation of the strip is that Hobbes is a real, living, breathing tiger, and everyone except Calvin is too jaded to see it. Calvin may typically be a mischievous, antisocial brat, but he is also a deep thinker, who cherishes time well-spent with friends and worries about death. Watterson gave us a protagonist who is wonderfully three-dimensional.<br><br>At least, until he <i>literally</i> becomes two-dimensional.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1461790540i/18911980.jpg" width="125" height="" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Seriously, stop reading this review and go read this book. But read it in the form of <i>The Essential Calvin and Hobbes</i>, which presents the Sunday pages in color. You won't regret it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5738987625608089679" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1061636816" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12140303302073409315">I think comic books like Calvin & Hobbes should be included in the self-help category. After all they make us feel good about life and ourselves. Bill Watterson's work is brilliant - the humor and the portrayal of the characters and the overall philosophy. And the beautiful illustrations pull you into the delightful world of a little rascal and his imaginary companion and their adventures. These comic series are more for adults with the underlying messages on what's wrong with the world and adul</span>
  <span id="freeText12140303302073409315" style="display:none">I think comic books like Calvin &amp; Hobbes should be included in the self-help category. After all they make us feel good about life and ourselves. Bill Watterson's work is brilliant - the humor and the portrayal of the characters and the overall philosophy. And the beautiful illustrations pull you into the delightful world of a little rascal and his imaginary companion and their adventures. These comic series are more for adults with the underlying messages on what's wrong with the world and adults in modern society, but the seriousness is so wonderfully disguised, that adults are taken back to their childhood. This book too has much to laugh at and learn from. There's plenty of Calvin-antics to entertain you. No, I wouldn't want to be in his mom's place. I deviously enjoy the heartache Calvin causes to her and am always rooting for Calvin to win and not be grounded. </span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/798905-mark?shelf=calvin-hobbes">calvin-hobbes</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer133851455" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10014134825641572629">The second collection (from 1988), this still has a few rough edges (Calvin’s a bit more sarky than usual, the design of Hobbes settles over the course of the collection) but plenty to recommend. From the transmogrifier transforming Calvin into a little tiger to the introduction - following a botched haircut by Hobbes that makes Calvin bald - of Tracer Bullet (“I’ve got eight slugs in me. One’s lead, the rest are bourbon”), from more school fun and more interplay with Susie, this rockets along. </span>
  <span id="freeText10014134825641572629" style="display:none">The second collection (from 1988), this still has a few rough edges (Calvin’s a bit more sarky than usual, the design of Hobbes settles over the course of the collection) but plenty to recommend. From the transmogrifier transforming Calvin into a little tiger to the introduction - following a botched haircut by Hobbes that makes Calvin bald - of Tracer Bullet (“I’ve got eight slugs in me. One’s lead, the rest are bourbon”), from more school fun and more interplay with Susie, this rockets along. This also features the ill raccoon that Calvin tries to help, which is one of the most poignant and beautifully crafted sequences I’ve ever read. Very highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10014134825641572629" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1441806070" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11648188955640253810">I recalled this as my least favorite of the Calvin and Hobbes books I grew up with and that estimate has not changed much. That is not to say this is a bad book; there are plenty of classic panels here and you can see Watterson revving up for the long epic stories of later volumes. But he is not yet there. This is Calvin in the doldrums, and even then it is a blast.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('/WVinfM2vfshHJgFODhi/i+ntAGsRDM+cEBgvdGLby5dkMMKimkU7t+cjo8kq5WFs47gXVx/sNvab5Wju40LqQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('dUy2ng5RHNexhIDBJAJA+3WljS8wzr089kcgIiuOXdrVuRcJdw61wk8Elks4kbeA6YzZc8D1PtlcaNU8QYg5XQ==')})
  }

Booth describes a winter\'s night years ago, when he and his friend, a bar owner named Herb Tooklander (Tookey), receive a visit from a distressed motorist named Gerard Lumley, whose vehicle had become stranded in a ferocious blizzard . . . with his wife and daughter still inside. At first critical of Lumley for driving in such weather, both men are horrified when they realise the Lumley\'s car is stranded in Jerusalem\'s Lot, widely regarded to have \'gone bad\'. Nevertheless, they still decide to drive out in a snow plough and attempt to save Lumley\'s family. Instead, they barely manage to save themselves.\n\nWidely regarded to be one of King\'s finest short stories—itself a sequel of sorts to what so many feel is perhaps his finest novel—\'One For The Road\' is the author working at the top of his form. For years people clamored for another visit to \'Salem\'s Lot\'. Well, here it is . . . a wintry little coda to one of King\'s scariest works. All the classic elements are here: an empty town, heavy weather, Yankee accents . . . and the monsters, of course. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer18373515884366069846">Bill Watterson (born William Boyd Watterson II) is an American cartoonist, and the author of the comic strip "Calvin and Hobbes". His career as a syndicated cartoonist ran from 1985 to 1995; he stopped drawing "Calvin and Hobbes" at the end of 1995 with a short statement to newspaper editors and his fans that he felt he had achieved all he could in the comic strip medium. During the early years of</span>
  <span id="freeText18373515884366069846" style="display:none">Bill Watterson (born William Boyd Watterson II) is an American cartoonist, and the author of the comic strip "Calvin and Hobbes". His career as a syndicated cartoonist ran from 1985 to 1995; he stopped drawing "Calvin and Hobbes" at the end of 1995 with a short statement to newspaper editors and his fans that he felt he had achieved all he could in the comic strip medium. During the early years of his career he produced several drawings and additional contributions for "Target: The Political Cartoon Quarterly". Watterson is known for his views on licensing and comic syndication, as well as for his reclusive nature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18373515884366069846" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
